<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Musical artist</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://queenlatifah.com">queenlatifah<wbr />.com</a></span> <span class="mw-valign-text-top noprint" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q1112005#P856" title="Edit this at Wikidata"><img alt="Edit this at Wikidata" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/10px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png" decoding="async" width="10" height="10" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/15px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/20px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Dana Elaine Owens</b> (born March 18, 1970), better known by the <a href="/wiki/Stage_name" title="Stage name">stage name</a> <b>Queen Latifah</b>, is an American actress, rapper and singer. She has received <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_nominations_received_by_Queen_Latifah" title="List of awards and nominations received by Queen Latifah">various accolades</a>, including a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Awards" title="Primetime Emmy Awards">Primetime Emmy Award</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Globe Award">Golden Globe Award</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild_Awards" title="Screen Actors Guild Awards">Screen Actors Guild Awards</a>, and two <a href="/wiki/NAACP_Image_Awards" title="NAACP Image Awards">NAACP Image Awards</a>, in addition to a nomination for an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a>. In 2006, she became the first hip hop artist to receive a star on the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Walk_of_Fame" title="Hollywood Walk of Fame">Hollywood Walk of Fame</a>.
</p><p>At age 19, Latifah released her debut album <i><a href="/wiki/All_Hail_the_Queen" title="All Hail the Queen">All Hail the Queen</a></i> (1989), featuring the hit single "Ladies First". Her second album <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_of_a_Sista%27" title="Nature of a Sista&#39;">Nature of a Sista'</a></i> (1991), was produced by Tommy Boy Records. Her third album, <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Reign_(album)" title="Black Reign (album)">Black Reign</a></i> (1993), became the first album by a solo female rapper to receive a gold certification from the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a> (RIAA),<sup id="cite_ref-all_music_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_music-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and spawned the single "<a href="/wiki/U.N.I.T.Y." title="U.N.I.T.Y.">U.N.I.T.Y.</a>", which was influential in raising awareness of violence against women and the objectification of Black female sexuality.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The track reached the <a href="/wiki/Top_40" title="Top 40">top 40</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>, and won a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a>. Her fourth album <i><a href="/wiki/Order_in_the_Court" title="Order in the Court">Order in the Court</a></i> (1998), was released with <a href="/wiki/Motown_Records_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Motown Records Group">Motown Records</a>. She has since released the albums <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dana_Owens_Album" title="The Dana Owens Album">The Dana Owens Album</a></i> (2004), <i><a href="/wiki/Trav%27lin%27_Light_(Queen_Latifah_album)" title="Trav&#39;lin&#39; Light (Queen Latifah album)">Trav'lin' Light</a></i> (2007), and <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_(Queen_Latifah_album)" title="Persona (Queen Latifah album)">Persona</a></i> (2009). 
</p><p>Latifah starred as Khadijah James on the <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> sitcom <i><a href="/wiki/Living_Single" title="Living Single">Living Single</a></i> from 1993 to 1998 and landed a leading role in the action film <i><a href="/wiki/Set_It_Off_(film)" title="Set It Off (film)">Set It Off</a></i> (1996). She created the daytime talk show <i><a href="/wiki/The_Queen_Latifah_Show" title="The Queen Latifah Show">The Queen Latifah Show</a></i>, which ran from 1999 to 2001, and again from 2013 to 2015, in syndication. Her portrayal of Matron "Mama" Morton in the musical film <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_(2002_film)" title="Chicago (2002 film)">Chicago</a></i> (2002), receiving a nomination for the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Supporting_Actress" title="Academy Award for Best Supporting Actress">Academy Award for Best Supporting Actress</a>. She has also starred or co-starred in the films <i><a href="/wiki/Bringing_Down_the_House_(film)" title="Bringing Down the House (film)">Bringing Down the House</a></i> (2003), <i><a href="/wiki/Taxi_(2004_film)" title="Taxi (2004 film)">Taxi</a></i> (2004), <i><a href="/wiki/Barbershop_2:_Back_in_Business" title="Barbershop 2: Back in Business">Barbershop 2: Back in Business</a></i> (2005), <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_Shop" title="Beauty Shop">Beauty Shop</a></i> (2005), <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Holiday_(2006_film)" title="Last Holiday (2006 film)">Last Holiday</a></i> (2006), <i><a href="/wiki/Hairspray_(2007_film)" title="Hairspray (2007 film)">Hairspray</a></i> (2007), <i><a href="/wiki/Joyful_Noise_(film)" title="Joyful Noise (film)">Joyful Noise</a></i> (2012), <i><a href="/wiki/22_Jump_Street" title="22 Jump Street">22 Jump Street</a></i> (2014), and <i><a href="/wiki/Girls_Trip" title="Girls Trip">Girls Trip</a></i> (2017); and provided voice work in the <i><a href="/wiki/Ice_Age_(franchise)" title="Ice Age (franchise)">Ice Age</a></i> film series.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Latifah received critical acclaim for her portrayal of blues singer <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Smith" title="Bessie Smith">Bessie Smith</a> in the <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Bessie_(film)" title="Bessie (film)">Bessie</a></i> (2015), which she co-produced, winning the <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award_for_Outstanding_Television_Movie" title="Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Television Movie">Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Television Movie</a>. From 2016 to 2019, she starred as Carlotta Brown in the musical drama series <i><a href="/wiki/Star_(TV_series)" title="Star (TV series)">Star</a></i>. In 2020, she portrayed <a href="/wiki/Hattie_McDaniel" title="Hattie McDaniel">Hattie McDaniel</a> in the miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/Hollywood_(miniseries)" title="Hollywood (miniseries)">Hollywood</a></i>. Since 2021, she has held the lead role on <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>'s revival of the action drama <i><a href="/wiki/The_Equalizer_(2021_TV_series)" title="The Equalizer (2021 TV series)">The Equalizer</a></i>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<p>Dana Elaine Owens was born in <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark, New Jersey</a>, on March 18, 1970, and lived primarily in <a href="/wiki/East_Orange,_New_Jersey" title="East Orange, New Jersey">East Orange, New Jersey</a>. She is the daughter of Rita Lamae (née Bray; d. 2018),<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> a teacher at <a href="/wiki/Irvington_High_School_(New_Jersey)" title="Irvington High School (New Jersey)">Irvington High School</a> (Dana's <a href="/wiki/Alma_mater" title="Alma mater">alma mater</a>), and Lancelot Amos Owens, a police officer.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>  Her parents divorced when she was ten.<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>She was raised in the <a href="/wiki/Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptist">Baptist</a> faith.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> She attended <a href="/wiki/Catholic_school" title="Catholic school">Catholic school</a> in Newark, New Jersey<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rd_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rd-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and Essex Catholic Girls' High School in Irvington but graduated from Irvington High School.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  After high school, she attended classes at <a href="/wiki/Borough_of_Manhattan_Community_College" title="Borough of Manhattan Community College">Borough of Manhattan Community College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>She found her stage name, <i>Latifah</i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl">لطيفة</span></span> <i>laţīfa</i>), meaning "delicate" and "very kind" in <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, in a book of Arabic names when she was eight.<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Always tall, the 5-foot-10-inch (1.78&#160;m) Dana was a <a href="/wiki/Power_forward_(basketball)" title="Power forward (basketball)">power forward</a> on her high school basketball team.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> She performed the number "<a href="/wiki/Home_(The_Wiz_song)" title="Home (The Wiz song)">Home</a>" from the musical <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wiz" title="The Wiz">The Wiz</a></i> in a grammar school play.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_career">Music career</span></h2>
<h3><span id="1988.E2.80.931989:_Career_beginnings"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1988–1989:_Career_beginnings">1988–1989: Career beginnings</span></h3>
<p>She began <a href="/wiki/Beat_boxing" class="mw-redirect" title="Beat boxing">beat boxing</a> for the hip-hop group Ladies Fresh and was an original member of the <a href="/wiki/Flavor_Unit" title="Flavor Unit">Flavor Unit</a>, which, at that time, was a crew of <a href="/wiki/MC" class="mw-redirect" title="MC">MCs</a> grouped around producer DJ King Gemini, who made a demo recording of Queen Latifah's rap Princess of the Posse. He gave the recording to <a href="/wiki/Fab_5_Freddy" class="mw-redirect" title="Fab 5 Freddy">Fab 5 Freddy</a>, the host of <i>Yo! MTV Raps</i>. The song got the attention of Tommy Boy Music employee <a href="/wiki/Dante_Ross" title="Dante Ross">Dante Ross</a>, who signed Latifah and in 1989 released  her first single, "Wrath of My Madness". More recent artists, like <a href="/wiki/Ice_Cube" title="Ice Cube">Ice Cube</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lil%27_Kim" title="Lil&#39; Kim">Lil' Kim</a>, would go on to sample Latifah's track in their songs, "Wrath of Kim's Madness" and "You Can't Play With My Yo-Yo" in later years. Latifah has a two-octave vocal range.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> She is considered a contralto, having the ability to both rap and sing.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1989.E2.80.932002:_Rap_and_hip-hop"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1989–2002:_Rap_and_hip-hop">1989–2002: Rap and hip-hop</span></h3>
<p>Latifah made her mark in hip-hop by rapping about the issues surrounding a black woman. Her songs had covered topics including <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>, harassment on the streets, and relationship problems.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
Freddy helped Latifah sign with <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Boy_Records" title="Tommy Boy Records">Tommy Boy Records</a>, which released Latifah's first album <i><a href="/wiki/All_Hail_the_Queen" title="All Hail the Queen">All Hail the Queen</a></i> in 1989, when she was nineteen.<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> That year, she appeared as Referee on the UK label Music of Life album <i>1989&#160;&#8211;&#32;The Hustlers Convention (live)</i>. She received a <a href="/wiki/Candace_Award" title="Candace Award">Candace Award</a> from the <a href="/wiki/National_Coalition_of_100_Black_Women" title="National Coalition of 100 Black Women">National Coalition of 100 Black Women</a> in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-jet92_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jet92-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The single "Ladies First" featuring <a href="/wiki/Monie_Love" title="Monie Love">Monie Love</a> became the first collaborative track by two female rappers not in a group.<sup id="cite_ref-vibe_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibe-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, she released the album <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Reign_(album)" title="Black Reign (album)">Black Reign</a></i>, which was certified Gold in the United States<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and produced the Grammy Award-winning song "<a href="/wiki/U.N.I.T.Y." title="U.N.I.T.Y.">U.N.I.T.Y.</a>" In 1998, co-produced by Ro Smith, now CEO of Def Ro Inc., she released her fourth hip-hop album <i><a href="/wiki/Order_in_the_Court" title="Order in the Court">Order in the Court</a></i>, which was released by <a href="/wiki/Motown_Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Motown Records">Motown Records</a>. Latifah was also a member of the hip-hop collective <a href="/wiki/Native_Tongues" title="Native Tongues">Native Tongues</a>.
</p><p>Latifah performed in the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXXII" title="Super Bowl XXXII">Super Bowl XXXII</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Super_Bowl_halftime_shows" title="List of Super Bowl halftime shows">halftime show</a>, making her the first rapper to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2003.E2.80.932009:_Change_to_traditional_singing"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2003–2009:_Change_to_traditional_singing">2003–2009: Change to traditional singing</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Latifah_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Queen_Latifah_1.jpg/220px-Queen_Latifah_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Queen_Latifah_1.jpg/330px-Queen_Latifah_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Queen_Latifah_1.jpg/440px-Queen_Latifah_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a><figcaption>Queen Latifah hosts LEAGUE National Awards and Recognition Luncheon 2008</figcaption></figure>
<p>After <i>Order in the Court</i>, Latifah shifted primarily to singing soul music and jazz standards, which she had used sparingly in her previous hip-hop-oriented records. In 2004, she released the <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul</a>/jazz standards <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dana_Owens_Album" title="The Dana Owens Album">The Dana Owens Album</a></i>. On July 11, 2007, Latifah sang at the famed <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Bowl" title="Hollywood Bowl">Hollywood Bowl</a> in Los Angeles as the headlining act in a live <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> concert. In front of a crowd of more than 12,400, she was backed by a 10-piece live orchestra and three backup vocalists, which was billed as the Queen Latifah Orchestra. Latifah performed new arrangements of standards including "<a href="/wiki/California_Dreaming" class="mw-redirect" title="California Dreaming">California Dreaming</a>", first made popular by 1960s icons <a href="/wiki/The_Mamas_%26_the_Papas" title="The Mamas &amp; the Papas">the Mamas &amp; the Papas</a>. Later in 2007, Latifah released an album titled <i><a href="/wiki/Trav%27lin%27_Light_(Queen_Latifah_album)" title="Trav&#39;lin&#39; Light (Queen Latifah album)">Trav'lin' Light</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Jill_Scott_(singer)" title="Jill Scott (singer)">Jill Scott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erykah_Badu" title="Erykah Badu">Erykah Badu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Sample" title="Joe Sample">Joe Sample</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Duke" title="George Duke">George Duke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_McBride" title="Christian McBride">Christian McBride</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">Stevie Wonder</a> made guest appearances.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The album was nominated for a Grammy in the "Best Traditional Pop Vocal Album" category.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, Latifah, along with the <a href="/wiki/NJPAC" class="mw-redirect" title="NJPAC">NJPAC</a> Jubilation Choir,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> recorded the title track on the album <i><a href="/wiki/Oh,_Happy_Day:_An_All-Star_Music_Celebration" class="mw-redirect" title="Oh, Happy Day: An All-Star Music Celebration">Oh, Happy Day: An All-Star Music Celebration</a></i>, covering the <a href="/wiki/Oh,_Happy_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Oh, Happy Day">song</a> that the <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hawkins_Singers" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin Hawkins Singers">Edwin Hawkins Singers</a> made popular in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2008.E2.80.93present:_Return_to_hip-hop"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2008–present:_Return_to_hip-hop">2008–present: Return to hip-hop</span></h3>
<p>In 2008, Latifah was asked if she would make another hip-hop album. She was quoted stating that the album was done already and it would be called <i>All Hail the Queen II</i>. The following year, in 2009, she released her album <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_(Queen_Latifah_album)" title="Persona (Queen Latifah album)">Persona</a></i>. The song "Cue the Rain" was released as the album's lead single.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> 2011 saw Queen Latifah sing "Who Can I Turn To" in a duet with Tony Bennett for his album <i><a href="/wiki/Duets_II_(Tony_Bennett_album)" title="Duets II (Tony Bennett album)">Duets II</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In January 2012, while appearing on <i><a href="/wiki/106_%26_Park" title="106 &amp; Park">106 &amp; Park</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Dolly_Parton" title="Dolly Parton">Dolly Parton</a>, to promote <i><a href="/wiki/Joyful_Noise_(film)" title="Joyful Noise (film)">Joyful Noise</a></i>, Latifah stated that she had been working on a new album.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_television">Film and television</span></h2>
<h3><span id="1991.E2.80.932001:_Early_career"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1991–2001:_Early_career">1991–2001: Early career</span></h3>
<p>She began her film career by having supporting roles in the 1991 and 1992 films <i><a href="/wiki/House_Party_2" title="House Party 2">House Party 2</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Juice_(1992_film)" title="Juice (1992 film)">Juice</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Jungle_Fever" title="Jungle Fever">Jungle Fever</a></i>. Moreover, she has guest starred in two episodes during the second season (1991–1992) of the <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> hit <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fresh_Prince_of_Bel-Air" title="The Fresh Prince of Bel-Air">The Fresh Prince of Bel-Air</a></i> and had a guest role as herself on a tv sitcom <i><a href="/wiki/Hangin%27_with_Mr._Cooper" title="Hangin&#39; with Mr. Cooper">Hangin' with Mr. Cooper</a></i> in 1993. From 1993 to 1998, Latifah had a starring role on <i><a href="/wiki/Living_Single" title="Living Single">Living Single</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">FOX</a> sitcom, which gained high ratings among black audiences; she also wrote and performed its theme song. Her mother Rita played her mother on-screen. Latifah appeared in the 1996 box-office hit, <i><a href="/wiki/Set_It_Off_(film)" title="Set It Off (film)">Set It Off</a></i>, and had a supporting role in the <a href="/wiki/Holly_Hunter" title="Holly Hunter">Holly Hunter</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Living_Out_Loud" title="Living Out Loud">Living Out Loud</a></i> (1998). She played the role of Thelma in the 1999 movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bone_Collector" title="The Bone Collector">The Bone Collector</a></i>, alongside <a href="/wiki/Denzel_Washington" title="Denzel Washington">Denzel Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angelina_Jolie" title="Angelina Jolie">Angelina Jolie</a>. She also had her own talk show, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Queen_Latifah_Show" title="The Queen Latifah Show">The Queen Latifah Show</a></i>, from 1999 to 2001 and revamped in 2013. On January 6, 2014, <i>The Queen Latifah Show</i> was renewed for a second season. However, on November 21, 2014, Sony Pictures Television canceled Latifah's show due to declining ratings. Production of the series closed down, taking effect on December 18, 2014, leaving new episodes that were broadcast until March 6, 2015.
</p>
<h3><span id="2002.E2.80.93present:_Mainstream_success"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2002–present:_Mainstream_success">2002–present: Mainstream success</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:QueenLatifah2009WATF_2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/QueenLatifah2009WATF_2.jpg/220px-QueenLatifah2009WATF_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/QueenLatifah2009WATF_2.jpg/330px-QueenLatifah2009WATF_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/QueenLatifah2009WATF_2.jpg/440px-QueenLatifah2009WATF_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4204" data-file-height="2746" /></a><figcaption>Queen Latifah performing at the "Kids Inaugural: We Are the Future" concert in 2009</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although Latifah had previously received some critical acclaim, she gained mainstream success after being cast as Matron "Mama" Morton in <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_(2002_film)" title="Chicago (2002 film)">Chicago</a></i>, a musical film that won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Picture" title="Academy Award for Best Picture">Academy Award for Best Picture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Latifah herself received the nomination for <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Supporting_Actress" title="Academy Award for Best Supporting Actress">Best Supporting Actress</a> for her role, but lost to co-star <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Zeta-Jones" title="Catherine Zeta-Jones">Catherine Zeta-Jones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Latifah is one of five <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop" class="mw-redirect" title="Hip hop">hip-hop</a>/<a href="/wiki/R%26B" class="mw-redirect" title="R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a> artists to receive an Academy Award nomination in an acting category. The others are <a href="/wiki/Will_Smith" title="Will Smith">Will Smith</a> (Best Actor, <i><a href="/wiki/Ali_(film)" title="Ali (film)">Ali</a></i>, 2001, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pursuit_of_Happyness" title="The Pursuit of Happyness">The Pursuit of Happyness</a></i>, 2006), <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Hudson" title="Jennifer Hudson">Jennifer Hudson</a> (Best Supporting Actress, <i><a href="/wiki/Dreamgirls_(film)" title="Dreamgirls (film)">Dreamgirls</a></i>, 2007), <a href="/wiki/Jamie_Foxx" title="Jamie Foxx">Jamie Foxx</a>, (Best Actor, <i><a href="/wiki/Ray_(film)" title="Ray (film)">Ray</a></i>, and Best Supporting Actor <i><a href="/wiki/Collateral_(film)" title="Collateral (film)">Collateral</a></i>, both in 2004, also winning the first) and <a href="/wiki/Mary_J._Blige" title="Mary J. Blige">Mary J. Blige</a> (Best Supporting Actress, <i><a href="/wiki/Mudbound_(film)" title="Mudbound (film)">Mudbound</a></i>, 2017).
</p><p>In 2003, she starred with <a href="/wiki/Steve_Martin" title="Steve Martin">Steve Martin</a> in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Bringing_Down_the_House_(movie)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bringing Down the House (movie)">Bringing Down the House</a></i>, which was a major success at the box office.<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> She also recorded a song "Do Your Thing" for the soundtrack. Since then, she has had both leading and supporting roles in a multitude of films that received varied critical and box office receptions, including films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Scary_Movie_3" title="Scary Movie 3">Scary Movie 3</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Barbershop_2:_Back_in_Business" title="Barbershop 2: Back in Business">Barbershop 2: Back in Business</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Taxi_(2004_film)" title="Taxi (2004 film)">Taxi</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kung_Faux" title="Kung Faux">Kung Faux</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_Shop" title="Beauty Shop">Beauty Shop</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Hairspray_(2007_film)" title="Hairspray (2007 film)">Hairspray</a></i>. In early 2006, Latifah appeared in a romantic comedy/drama entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Holiday_(2006_film)" title="Last Holiday (2006 film)">Last Holiday</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Film critic <a href="/wiki/Richard_Roeper" title="Richard Roeper">Richard Roeper</a> stated that "this is the Queen Latifah performance I've been waiting for ever since she broke into movies".<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Also in 2006, Latifah voiced <a href="/wiki/Ellie_(Ice_Age)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellie (Ice Age)">Ellie</a>, a friendly <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a>, in the animated film, <i><a href="/wiki/Ice_Age:_The_Meltdown" title="Ice Age: The Meltdown">Ice Age: The Meltdown</a></i> (her first voice appearance in an animated film), and appeared in the drama <i><a href="/wiki/Stranger_than_Fiction_(2006_film)" title="Stranger than Fiction (2006 film)">Stranger Than Fiction</a></i>.
</p><p>The summer of 2007 brought Latifah triple success in the big-screen version of the Broadway smash hit <i><a href="/wiki/Hairspray_(2007_film)" title="Hairspray (2007 film)">Hairspray</a></i>, in which she acted, sang, and danced. The film rated highly with critics. It starred, among others, <a href="/wiki/John_Travolta" title="John Travolta">John Travolta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Pfeiffer" title="Michelle Pfeiffer">Michelle Pfeiffer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allison_Janney" title="Allison Janney">Allison Janney</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Marsden" title="James Marsden">James Marsden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Walken" title="Christopher Walken">Christopher Walken</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zac_Efron" title="Zac Efron">Zac Efron</a>. Also in 2007, she portrayed an <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>-positive woman in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Life_Support_(film)" title="Life Support (film)">Life Support</a></i>, a role for which she garnered her first <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Globe Award">Golden Globe Award</a>, <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild" title="Screen Actors Guild">Screen Actors Guild</a> Award and an <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Emmy Award">Emmy</a><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> nomination. For her work, Queen Latifah received a star on the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Walk_of_Fame" title="Hollywood Walk of Fame">Hollywood Walk of Fame</a>, on January 4, 2006, located at 6915 Hollywood Blvd.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Super_Bowl_44_Queen_Latifah_God_Bless_America_(4344821074)_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Super_Bowl_44_Queen_Latifah_God_Bless_America_%284344821074%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Super_Bowl_44_Queen_Latifah_God_Bless_America_%284344821074%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="265" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Super_Bowl_44_Queen_Latifah_God_Bless_America_%284344821074%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Super_Bowl_44_Queen_Latifah_God_Bless_America_%284344821074%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Super_Bowl_44_Queen_Latifah_God_Bless_America_%284344821074%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Super_Bowl_44_Queen_Latifah_God_Bless_America_%284344821074%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="625" data-file-height="752" /></a><figcaption>Latifah performing <a href="/wiki/God_Bless_America" title="God Bless America">God Bless America</a> at <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XLIV" title="Super Bowl XLIV">Super Bowl XLIV</a> in 2010</figcaption></figure>
<p>Queen Latifah produced the 2007 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Perfect_Holiday" title="The Perfect Holiday">The Perfect Holiday</a></i>. In addition to producing the film, Latifah starred alongside <a href="/wiki/Terrence_Howard" title="Terrence Howard">Terrence Howard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morris_Chestnut" title="Morris Chestnut">Morris Chestnut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabrielle_Union" title="Gabrielle Union">Gabrielle Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Murphy_(actor)" title="Charlie Murphy (actor)">Charles Q. Murphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jill_Marie_Jones" title="Jill Marie Jones">Jill Marie Jones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Faizon_Love" title="Faizon Love">Faizon Love</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-upcoming_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upcoming-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, Latifah appeared in the crime comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Mad_Money_(film)" title="Mad Money (film)">Mad Money</a></i> opposite <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a>–winner <a href="/wiki/Diane_Keaton" title="Diane Keaton">Diane Keaton</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Katie_Holmes" title="Katie Holmes">Katie Holmes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ted_Danson" title="Ted Danson">Ted Danson</a>. She appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Live" title="Saturday Night Live">Saturday Night Live</a></i> on October 4, 2008, as moderator <a href="/wiki/Gwen_Ifill" title="Gwen Ifill">Gwen Ifill</a> in a comedic sketch depicting the <a href="/wiki/United_States_vice-presidential_debate,_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="United States vice-presidential debate, 2008">vice-presidential debate</a> between then-Senator <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a> and then-Governor <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Palin" title="Sarah Palin">Sarah Palin</a><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and played in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Life_of_Bees_(film)" title="The Secret Life of Bees (film)">The Secret Life of Bees</a></i>. In 2009, Latifah was a presenter at the <a href="/wiki/81st_Academy_Awards" title="81st Academy Awards">81st Academy Awards</a>, presenting the segment honoring film professionals who had died during 2008 and singing "<a href="/wiki/I%27ll_Be_Seeing_You_(song)" title="I&#39;ll Be Seeing You (song)">I'll Be Seeing You</a>" during the montage. Latifah spoke at <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a>'s memorial service in Los Angeles. She also hosted the <a href="/wiki/2010_People%27s_Choice_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="2010 People&#39;s Choice Awards">2010 People's Choice Awards</a>. Latifah sang "<a href="/wiki/America_the_Beautiful" title="America the Beautiful">America the Beautiful</a>" at <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XLIV" title="Super Bowl XLIV">Super Bowl XLIV</a> hosted in <a href="/wiki/Miami,_Florida" class="mw-redirect" title="Miami, Florida">Miami, Florida</a>, on February 7, 2010, with <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Underwood" title="Carrie Underwood">Carrie Underwood</a>. Latifah hosted the <a href="/wiki/BET_Awards_2010" title="BET Awards 2010">2010 BET Awards</a> on June 27, 2010. She starred with <a href="/wiki/Dolly_Parton" title="Dolly Parton">Dolly Parton</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Joyful_Noise_(film)" title="Joyful Noise (film)">Joyful Noise</a></i> (2012).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In June 2011, Latifah received an honorary doctorate degree in Humane Letters from <a href="/wiki/Delaware_State_University" title="Delaware State University">Delaware State University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dover,_Delaware" title="Dover, Delaware">Dover, Delaware</a>. On September 16, 2013, Latifah premiered her own syndicated daytime television show titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Queen_Latifah_Show" title="The Queen Latifah Show">The Queen Latifah Show</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> On January 26, 2014, Latifah officiated the weddings of 33 same-sex and opposite-sex couples during a performance of "<a href="/wiki/Same_Love" title="Same Love">Same Love</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Macklemore" title="Macklemore">Macklemore</a> at the <a href="/wiki/56th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="56th Annual Grammy Awards">56th Annual Grammy Awards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, Latifah received a <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award_for_Outstanding_Lead_Actress_in_a_Limited_or_Anthology_Series_or_Movie" title="Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Lead Actress in a Limited or Anthology Series or Movie">Best Actress Emmy</a> nomination for her lead role as <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Smith" title="Bessie Smith">Bessie Smith</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Bessie_(film)" title="Bessie (film)">Bessie</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> film which received a total of 12 <a href="/wiki/Emmy" class="mw-redirect" title="Emmy">Emmy</a> nominations.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 26, 2017, <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a> announced that Latifah will be an executive producer for the third season of the slasher television series <i><a href="/wiki/Scream_(TV_series)" title="Scream (TV series)">Scream</a></i>. The show will undergo a reboot with a new cast and <a href="/wiki/Brett_Matthews" title="Brett Matthews">Brett Matthews</a> serving as show runner. In addition, Matthews, Shakim Compere and Yaneley Arty will also be credited as executive producers for the series under <a href="/wiki/Flavor_Unit_Entertainment" title="Flavor Unit Entertainment">Flavor Unit Entertainment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Screenrant.com_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Screenrant.com-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Deadline_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deadline-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> On June 24, 2019, it was confirmed that the third season is scheduled to premiere over three nights on <a href="/wiki/VH1" title="VH1">VH1</a>, starting from July 8, 2019. The third season titled <i>Scream: Resurrection</i> premiered on July 8, 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Latifah played the sea witch <a href="/wiki/Ursula_(The_Little_Mermaid)" title="Ursula (The Little Mermaid)">Ursula</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Little_Mermaid_Live!" title="The Little Mermaid Live!">The Little Mermaid Live!</a></i>. Although the production itself was not well received, critics widely praised Latifah's performance,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> with <i>The Hollywood Reporter</i> calling her performance "the best moment of the evening".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2021, CBS premiered the new active TV series, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Equalizer_(2021_TV_series)" title="The Equalizer (2021 TV series)">The Equalizer</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Reboot_(fiction)" title="Reboot (fiction)">reboot</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Equalizer_(1985_TV_series)" title="The Equalizer (1985 TV series)">the 1980s detective series of the same name</a>, starring Latifah in the lead role (renamed as Robyn for her version).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> More recently, she signed a deal with <a href="/wiki/Audible_(service)" title="Audible (service)">Audible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistry">Artistry</span></h2>
<p>Latifah's music usually contains <a href="/wiki/Hip-hop" class="mw-redirect" title="Hip-hop">hip-hop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">gospel</a> and has the elements of R&amp;B, soul, and <a href="/wiki/Dance_music" title="Dance music">dance</a>. She possesses a two-octave vocal range. Queen Latifah is a <a href="/wiki/Contralto" title="Contralto">contralto</a>, and she has the ability to rap and sing. Her biggest musical influences are <a href="/wiki/EPMD" title="EPMD">EPMD</a>, <a href="/wiki/KRS-One" title="KRS-One">KRS-One</a>, <a href="/wiki/LL_Cool_J" title="LL Cool J">LL Cool J</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_Enemy_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Enemy (band)">Public Enemy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Run%E2%80%93D.M.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Run–D.M.C.">Run–D.M.C.</a><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> She also cites <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Smith" title="Bessie Smith">Bessie Smith</a> as one of her influences.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Al Hail the Queen</i> features hip-hop, reggae, <a href="/wiki/Soul_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Soul (music)">soulful</a> back-up vocals, <a href="/wiki/Hard_bop" title="Hard bop">boppish</a> <a href="/wiki/Scat_singing" title="Scat singing">scatting</a>, snappy horn back-ups, and <a href="/wiki/House_music" title="House music">house music</a>. She described the work as "a creative outlet... and sometimes it can become like a newspaper that people read with their ears."<sup id="cite_ref-csmonitor_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csmonitor-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early in her career, Queen Latifah's lyrics were described as woman-centered and <a href="/wiki/Afrocentrism" title="Afrocentrism">Afrocentric</a>. The rapper often used Afrocentric attires during public appearances and music videos, looks that became her trademark.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 1990, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span> Michelle Wallace described her art as "politically sophisticated", which "seems worlds apart from the adolescent, buffoonish sex orientation of most rap."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> For <i><a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a></i>, her "strong, intelligent, no-nonsense" persona made her "arguably the first MC who could properly be described as feminist".<sup id="cite_ref-all_music_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_music-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Queen Latifah did not identify as a feminist at the time, and expressed that her music was not exclusive for the female audience.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> On the topic, author <a href="/wiki/Tricia_Rose" title="Tricia Rose">Tricia Rose</a> wrote that Black female rappers likely did not identify with feminism <a href="/wiki/Second-wave_feminism" title="Second-wave feminism">during that time</a> because it was perceived as a movement that focused primarily on white women's issues.<sup id="cite_ref-tricia_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tricia-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Products_and_endorsements">Products and endorsements</span></h2>
<p>Latifah is a celebrity spokesperson for <a href="/wiki/CoverGirl" title="CoverGirl">CoverGirl</a> cosmetics, Curvation women's underwear, <a href="/wiki/Pizza_Hut" title="Pizza Hut">Pizza Hut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Craig,_Inc." title="Jenny Craig, Inc.">Jenny Craig</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> She represents her own line of cosmetics for women of color called the CoverGirl Queen Collection.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Latifah has also launched a perfume line called "Queen" and "Queen of Hearts". On May 23, 2018, Latifah was named the <a href="/wiki/Ship_sponsor" title="Ship sponsor">godmother</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carnival_Cruise_Lines" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnival Cruise Lines">Carnival Cruise Lines'</a> vessel <a href="/wiki/Carnival_Horizon" title="Carnival Horizon">Carnival Horizon</a>. Apart from singing, Queen Latifah has written a book on confidence and self-respect called <i>Ladies First: Revelations of a Strong Woman.</i><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<p>Raised in <a href="/wiki/East_Orange,_New_Jersey" title="East Orange, New Jersey">East Orange, New Jersey</a>, Latifah has been a resident of <a href="/wiki/Colts_Neck,_New_Jersey" class="mw-redirect" title="Colts Neck, New Jersey">Colts Neck, New Jersey</a>; <a href="/wiki/Rumson,_New_Jersey" title="Rumson, New Jersey">Rumson, New Jersey</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills,_California" title="Beverly Hills, California">Beverly Hills, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Latifah's older brother, Lancelot Jr., was killed in 1992 in an accident involving a motorcycle that Latifah had purchased for him.<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> A 2006 interview revealed that Latifah still wears the key to the motorcycle around her neck,<sup id="cite_ref-actors_9-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actors-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> visible throughout her performance in her sitcom <i><a href="/wiki/Living_Single" title="Living Single">Living Single</a></i>. In 1995, Latifah was the victim of a <a href="/wiki/Carjacking" title="Carjacking">carjacking</a>, which also resulted in the shooting of her boyfriend, Sean Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1996, she was arrested and charged with possession of marijuana and possession of a loaded handgun.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, she was arrested for driving under the influence in Los Angeles County.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> She was placed on three years' probation after being convicted.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 21, 2018, her mother, actress Rita Owens, died due to heart failure, an issue she had been battling since 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Latifah long refused to address speculation around her sexuality and personal life, telling <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> in 2008 that "I don't feel like I need to share my personal life, and I don't care if people think I'm gay or not".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/BET_Awards_2021" title="BET Awards 2021">BET Awards 2021</a>, during her acceptance speech for the Lifetime Achievement Award, she publicly acknowledged her partner Eboni Nichols and son Rebel for the first time, ending the speech with "happy <a href="/wiki/Pride_Month" title="Pride Month">Pride</a>!"<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the January 2020 season 6, episode 4 of <i><a href="/wiki/Finding_Your_Roots" title="Finding Your Roots">Finding Your Roots</a></i> titled "This Land Is My Land", Latifah learned that her family were descended from a line of <a href="/wiki/Free_Negro" title="Free Negro">freed Negroes</a>, since her ancestors were listed by name in the U.S. pre–Civil War census of 1860 in Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Slaves were almost never listed by name in pre–U.S. Civil War censuses. Latifah also learned the exact date her ancestors became free which was October 1, 1792, the date her second earliest known ancestor, a woman named "Jug" or Juggy Owens, was emancipated from slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feud_with_Foxy_Brown">Feud with Foxy Brown</span></h3>
<p>Disagreements between <a href="/wiki/Foxy_Brown_(rapper)" title="Foxy Brown (rapper)">Foxy Brown</a> and Queen Latifah began in mid-1996, where media reports indicated that Brown was a prime target of Latifah's <a href="/wiki/Diss_(music)" title="Diss (music)">diss record</a> "Name Callin<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>", which was featured on the <a href="/wiki/Set_It_Off_(soundtrack)" class="mw-redirect" title="Set It Off (soundtrack)"><i>Set It Off</i> soundtrack</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In response, Brown made allegations of Latifah "checking her out" at musical events and further questioned Latifah's sexuality in various public radio interviews. In 1998, Brown released a diss record titled "10% Dis", where she continually questioned Latifah's sexuality and accused her of being jealous.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By late spring of 1998, Latifah responded to Brown through another diss record titled "Name Callin' Part II".<sup id="cite_ref-daveyd_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daveyd-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-egotrip_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egotrip-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> On the record, Latifah disses Brown about her heavy reliance on sex appeal, in which she implies that Brown has to rely on skimpy outfits to hide her "half-assed flow".<sup id="cite_ref-daveyd_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daveyd-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Foxy Brown retaliated via a response-diss record titled "Talk to Me", in which Brown made fun of the ratings of Latifah's <a href="/wiki/The_Queen_Latifah_Show" title="The Queen Latifah Show">television talk show</a> and went on to make various homophobic remarks to both Latifah and then-newcomer <a href="/wiki/Queen_Pen" title="Queen Pen">Queen Pen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A significant part of media dubbed Latifah "the winner" of the feud.<sup id="cite_ref-egotrip_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egotrip-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Hip-hop magazine <a href="/wiki/Ego_trip_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ego trip (magazine)"><i>Ego Trip</i></a> stated that Latifah won the feud with her diss record "Name Callin' Part II" and added that she showed that "the lady's still first", in reference to Latifah's 1990 single, "Ladies First".<sup id="cite_ref-egotrip_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egotrip-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In 2000, Brown and Latifah reconciled; to prove that the truce was real, Brown performed her song "Na Na Be Like" on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Queen_Latifah_Show" title="The Queen Latifah Show">The Queen Latifah Show</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<p>Often cited as one of the best female rappers,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Queen Latifah achieved groundbreaking success<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> in the late 1980s and early 1990s, and became what <a href="/wiki/Pitchfork_(website)" title="Pitchfork (website)"><i>Pitchfork</i></a> considered as the "most recognizable female rapper" of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_hip_hop" title="Golden age hip hop">golden era of hip hop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a></i> writer Steve Huey stated that Latifah was "certainly not the first female rapper, but she was the first one to become a bona fide star."<sup id="cite_ref-all_music_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_music-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In the book <i><a href="/wiki/Notable_Black_American_Women" title="Notable Black American Women">Notable Black American Women</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Jessie_Carney_Smith" title="Jessie Carney Smith">Jessie Carney Smith</a> hailed her as "rap's first feminist" and "one of the few women to make a mark in the male-dominated field of rap music".<sup id="cite_ref-book_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-book-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i> called her "one of the major forerunners for women in modern hip-hop,"<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> referred to her as a "pioneer of female rap".<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout her career, several media publications have referred to her as the "<a href="/wiki/Honorific_nicknames_in_popular_music#L" title="Honorific nicknames in popular music">Queen of Rap</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-csmonitor_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csmonitor-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cbs_news_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbs_news-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/New_York_magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="New York magazine"><i>New York</i> magazine</a> (1990) via editor <a href="/wiki/Dinitia_Smith" title="Dinitia Smith">Dinitia Smith</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-book_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-book-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> as well as "Queen of Hip Hop".<sup id="cite_ref-cbs_news_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbs_news-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-guardian_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Latifah became the first solo female rapper to receive a <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">RIAA certification</a> for an album (<i><a href="/wiki/Black_Reign_(album)" title="Black Reign (album)">Black Reign</a></i>), a commercial breakthrough that the <i>AllMusic</i> editor considered as creating a path for "a talented crew of women rappers to make their own way onto the charts as the 90s progressed".<sup id="cite_ref-all_music_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_music-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Her breakthrough also helped place New Jersey on the hip hop map.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 1998, she performed in the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXXII" title="Super Bowl XXXII">Super Bowl XXXII</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Super_Bowl_halftime_shows" title="List of Super Bowl halftime shows">halftime show</a>, making her the first rapper to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to an <i><a href="/wiki/African_American_Review" title="African American Review">African American Review</a></i> journal, her <a href="/wiki/Afrocentrism" title="Afrocentrism">afrocentric</a> feminist music video for "Ladies First" presented a "televisual moment" and disrupted the continuity of sexism and racism that dominated the music videos at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The song was listed on the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a>'s <a href="/wiki/500_Songs_That_Shaped_Rock_and_Roll" class="mw-redirect" title="500 Songs That Shaped Rock and Roll">500 Songs That Shaped Rock and Roll</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and was one of the firsts texts to address the declining standards of male-female relationships in community life.<sup id="cite_ref-tricia_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tricia-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Author <a href="/wiki/Tricia_Rose" title="Tricia Rose">Tricia Rose</a> expressed that it "offered hip-hop for the development of pro-female pro-black diasporas political consciousness."<sup id="cite_ref-tricia_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tricia-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Consequence_(publication)" title="Consequence (publication)">Consequence</a></i>, Okla Jones noted that the song "<a href="/wiki/U.N.I.T.Y." title="U.N.I.T.Y.">U.N.I.T.Y.</a>"—which lyrics confront slurs against women in hip-hop culture and address other types of disrespect—created a path for future female rappers to be "their authentic selves".<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acting">Acting</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vibe_(magazine)" title="Vibe (magazine)"><i>Vibe</i> magazine</a> has noted her as the first female rapper to cross over into TV &amp; film,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> as an artist that "broke barriers and set standards" for Black women in music to follow, and cited her as the "First Lady of Hip-Hop".<sup id="cite_ref-vibe_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibe-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> For her performance as Matron "Mama" Morton in <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_(2002_film)" title="Chicago (2002 film)">Chicago</a></i>, Latifah earned a nomination for the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Supporting_Actress" title="Academy Award for Best Supporting Actress">Academy Award for Best Supporting Actress</a>, becoming the first woman in hip hop to earn an Oscar nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span></h3>
<p>Queen Latifah has been cited as an influence on R&amp;B, soul, and hip-hop artists, such as <a href="/wiki/Eve_(rapper)" title="Eve (rapper)">Eve</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Da_Brat" title="Da Brat">Da Brat</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lil%27_Kim" title="Lil&#39; Kim">Lil' Kim</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fugees" title="Fugees">Fugees</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jill_Scott_(singer)" title="Jill Scott (singer)">Jill Scott</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lauryn_Hill" title="Lauryn Hill">Lauryn Hill</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Missy_Elliott" title="Missy Elliott">Missy Elliott</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Remy_Ma" title="Remy Ma">Remy Ma</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Queen" title="Ivy Queen">Ivy Queen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Foxy_Brown_(rapper)" title="Foxy Brown (rapper)">Foxy Brown</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ms._Dynamite" title="Ms. Dynamite">Ms. Dynamite</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Naughty_by_Nature" title="Naughty by Nature">Naughty by Nature</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rapsody" title="Rapsody">Rapsody</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Megan_Thee_Stallion" title="Megan Thee Stallion">Megan Thee Stallion</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> as well as actors <a href="/wiki/Michael_K._Williams" title="Michael K. Williams">Michael K. Williams</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Keke_Palmer" title="Keke Palmer">Keke Palmer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vin_Diesel" title="Vin Diesel">Vin Diesel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> and author <a href="/wiki/Jason_Reynolds" title="Jason Reynolds">Jason Reynolds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Playwright <a href="/wiki/Lin-Manuel_Miranda" title="Lin-Manuel Miranda">Lin-Manuel Miranda</a> has stated that Latifah inspired the portrayal of <a href="/wiki/Angelica_Schuyler_Church" title="Angelica Schuyler Church">Angelica Schuyler</a> in the musical <i><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_(musical)" title="Hamilton (musical)">Hamilton</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In 2020, <i><a href="/wiki/Vogue_(magazine)" title="Vogue (magazine)">Vogue</a></i> editor Janelle Okwodu considered her a <a href="/wiki/Fashion_icon" title="Fashion icon">fashion icon</a> that "helped to start a conversation about body image that continues to this day", crediting her among the first artists that pioneered the "climate of size inclusivity and muses of all shapes".<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accolades">Accolades</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_nominations_received_by_Queen_Latifah" title="List of awards and nominations received by Queen Latifah">List of awards and nominations received by Queen Latifah</a></div>
<p>Queen Latifah became the first female hip-hop recording artist to get nominated for an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Oscar</a>. In 2003, Queen Latifah was awarded Artist of the Year by <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a> Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, Latifah became the first hip-hop artist to receive a star on the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Walk_of_Fame" title="Hollywood Walk of Fame">Hollywood Walk of Fame</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> and was also inducted into the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Hall_of_Fame" title="New Jersey Hall of Fame">New Jersey Hall of Fame</a> in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In her music career, Queen Latifah has sold nearly 2 million albums in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Root_(magazine)" title="The Root (magazine)">The Root</a></i> ranked her at number 35 on <i>The Root</i> 100 list.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, <a href="/wiki/American_Black_Film_Festival" title="American Black Film Festival">American Black Film Festival</a> honored Latifah with the Entertainment Icon award.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In 2018, she received an honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Fine_Arts" title="Doctor of Fine Arts">Doctor of Fine Arts</a> Degree by the <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In 2019, Harvard University awarded the <a href="/wiki/W._E._B._Du_Bois" title="W. E. B. Du Bois">W. E. B. Du Bois</a> Medal to Queen Latifah for cultural contributions.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> In 2023, Queen Latifah's debut album <i>All Hail the Queen,</i> was added into the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress's</a> <a href="/wiki/National_Recording_Registry" title="National Recording Registry">National Recording Registry</a>, making it the earliest female rap recording to enter the National Recording Registry,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> and made her the second female hip-hop recording artist to have her music included after <a href="/wiki/Lauryn_Hill" title="Lauryn Hill">Lauryn Hill</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> however some outlets incorrectly reported her as the first to accomplish the feat.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>She is a recipient of a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a> from six nominations, a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Globe Award">Golden Globe Award</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild_Awards" title="Screen Actors Guild Awards">Screen Actors Guild Awards</a> from five nominations, two <a href="/wiki/NAACP_Image_Awards" title="NAACP Image Awards">NAACP Image Awards</a> from thirteen nominations, one <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Primetime Emmy Award">Primetime Emmy Award</a> from three nominations, and an Academy Award nomination. In 2021, she received the <a href="/wiki/BET_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="BET Award">BET</a> Lifetime Achievement Award and was the first rapper female or male to be awarded.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discography">Discography</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Queen_Latifah_discography" title="Queen Latifah discography">Queen Latifah discography</a></div>
<p><b>Studio albums</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/All_Hail_the_Queen" title="All Hail the Queen">All Hail the Queen</a></i> (1989)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Nature_of_a_Sista%27" title="Nature of a Sista&#39;">Nature of a Sista'</a></i> (1991)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Black_Reign_(album)" title="Black Reign (album)">Black Reign</a></i> (1993)</li>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Persona_(Queen_Latifah_album)" title="Persona (Queen Latifah album)">Persona</a></i> (2009)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tours">Tours</span></h2>
<p>Latifah, <a href="/wiki/Jill_Scott_(singer)" title="Jill Scott (singer)">Jill Scott</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erykah_Badu" title="Erykah Badu">Erykah Badu</a> joined to create and own the rights to the Sugar Water Festival Tour, LLC. All three singers toured together while inviting music duo <a href="/wiki/Floetry" title="Floetry">Floetry</a> in 2005 and singer <a href="/wiki/Kelis" title="Kelis">Kelis</a> in 2006 as opening acts. Comedian/actress <a href="/wiki/Mo%27Nique" title="Mo&#39;Nique">Mo'Nique</a> served as host for the 2006 Sugar Water Tour.
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Sugar_Water_Festival_Tour" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar Water Festival Tour">Sugar Water Festival Tour</a></i> (2005–06)</li>
<li><i>Travlin' Light Tour</i> (2007)</li></ul>
